<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Moons of Uranus</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The natural satellites of the planet Uranus</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_moons.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1150" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Uranus_moons.jpg/300px-Uranus_moons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Uranus_moons.jpg/450px-Uranus_moons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Uranus_moons.jpg/600px-Uranus_moons.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_moons.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranus and its six largest moons compared at their proper relative sizes and relative positions. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Puck_(moon)" title="Puck (moon)">Puck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, the seventh planet of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, has 27 known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, all of which are named after characters from the works of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">Alexander Pope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-1">[1]</a></sup> Uranus's moons are divided into three groups: thirteen inner moons, five major moons, and nine irregular moons. The inner moons are small dark bodies that share common properties and origins with <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">Uranus's rings</a>. The five major moons are massive enough to have reached <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, and four of them show signs of internally driven processes such as canyon formation and volcanism on their surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> The largest of these five, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, is 1,578 km in diameter and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons" title="List of moons">eighth-largest</a> moon in the Solar System, and about one-twentieth the mass the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. The orbits of the regular moons are nearly <a href="/wiki/Coplanarity" title="Coplanarity">coplanar</a> with Uranus's equator, which is tilted 97.77° to its orbit. Uranus's irregular moons have elliptical and strongly inclined (mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_direct_motion" title="Retrograde and direct motion">retrograde</a>) orbits at large distances from the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> discovered the first two moons, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>, in 1787, and the other three <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">ellipsoidal</a> moons were discovered in 1851 by <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">William Lassell</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>) and in 1948 by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Gerard Kuiper</a> (<a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-1">[1]</a></sup> These five have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planemo" title="Planemo">planetary mass</a>, and so would be considered (dwarf) planets if they were in direct orbit about the Sun. The remaining moons were discovered after 1985, either during the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> flyby mission or with the aid of advanced <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>-based telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first two moons to be discovered were <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>, which were spotted by Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> on January 11, 1787, six years after he had discovered the planet itself. Later, Herschel thought he had discovered up to six moons (see below) and perhaps even a ring. For nearly 50 years, Herschel's instrument was the only one with which the moons had been seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In the 1840s, better instruments and a more favorable position of Uranus in the sky led to sporadic indications of satellites additional to Titania and Oberon. Eventually, the next two moons, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, were discovered by <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">William Lassell</a> in 1851.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell1851_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lassell1851-5">[5]</a></sup> The Roman numbering scheme of Uranus's moons was in a state of flux for a considerable time, and publications hesitated between Herschel's designations (where Titania and Oberon are Uranus II and IV) and William Lassell's (where they are sometimes I and II).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> With the confirmation of Ariel and Umbriel, Lassell numbered the moons I through IV from Uranus outward, and this finally stuck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell1851b_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lassell1851b-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1852, Herschel's son <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a> gave the four then-known moons their names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1949_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1949-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>No other discoveries were made for almost another century. In 1948, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Gerard Kuiper</a> at the <a href="/wiki/McDonald_Observatory" title="McDonald Observatory">McDonald Observatory</a> discovered the smallest and the last of the five large, spherical moons, <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1949_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1949-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes1948b_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes1948b-9">[9]</a></sup> Decades later, the flyby of the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> space probe in January 1986 led to the discovery of ten further inner moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> Another satellite, <a href="/wiki/Perdita_(moon)" title="Perdita (moon)">Perdita</a>, was discovered in 1999<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_7171_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUC_7171-10">[10]</a></sup> after studying old <i>Voyager</i> photographs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka2001,_Voyager_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka2001,_Voyager-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranus was the last giant planet without any known <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">irregular moons</a>, but since 1997 nine distant irregular moons have been identified using ground-based telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> Two more small inner moons, <a href="/wiki/Cupid_(moon)" title="Cupid (moon)">Cupid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mab_(moon)" title="Mab (moon)">Mab</a>, were discovered using the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showalter_Lissauer_2006_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Showalter_Lissauer_2006-12">[12]</a></sup> As of 2016, the moon <a href="/wiki/Margaret_(moon)" title="Margaret (moon)">Margaret</a> was the last Uranian moon discovered, and its characteristics were published in October 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_8217_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUC_8217-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outer_planet_moons.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="824" data-file-width="1471" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Outer_planet_moons.png/220px-Outer_planet_moons.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Outer_planet_moons.png/330px-Outer_planet_moons.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Outer_planet_moons.png/440px-Outer_planet_moons.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Outer_planet_moons.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The number of moons known for each of the four outer planets up to July 2018. Uranus currently has 27 known satellites.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spurious_moons">Spurious moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spurious moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After Herschel discovered <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a> on January 11, 1787, he subsequently believed that he had observed four other moons: two on January 18 and February 9, 1790, and two more on February 28 and March 26, 1794. It was thus believed for many decades thereafter that Uranus had a system of six satellites, though the four latter moons were never confirmed by any other astronomer. <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">Lassell</a>'s observations of 1851, in which he discovered <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, however, failed to support Herschel's observations; Ariel and Umbriel, which Herschel certainly ought to have seen if he had seen any satellites beside Titania and Oberon, did not correspond to any of Herschel's four additional satellites in orbital characteristics. Herschel's four spurious satellites were thought to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_period" title="Sidereal period">sidereal periods</a> of 5.89 days (interior to Titania), 10.96 days (between Titania and Oberon), 38.08 days, and 107.69 days (exterior to Oberon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes1994_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes1994-14">[14]</a></sup> It was therefore concluded that Herschel's four satellites were spurious, probably arising from the misidentification of faint stars in the vicinity of Uranus as satellites, and the credit for the discovery of Ariel and Umbriel was given to Lassell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denning1881_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denning1881-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naming_of_moons" title="Naming of moons">Naming of moons</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Name_conflicts_with_minor_planets" title="Name conflicts with minor planets">Name conflicts with minor planets</a></div>
<p>Although the first two Uranian moons were discovered in 1787, they were not named until 1852, a year after two more moons had been discovered. The responsibility for naming was taken by <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">John Herschel</a>, son of the discoverer of Uranus. Herschel, instead of assigning names from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, named the moons after magical spirits in <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a>: the fairies Oberon and Titania from <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sylphs" title="Sylphs">sylphs</a> Ariel and Umbriel from <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">Alexander Pope</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rape_of_the_Lock" title="The Rape of the Lock">The Rape of the Lock</a></i> (Ariel is also a sprite in Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">The Tempest</a></i>). The reasoning was presumably that Uranus, as god of the sky and air, would be attended by spirits of the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Subsequent names, rather than continuing the airy spirits theme (only <a href="/wiki/Puck_(moon)" title="Puck (moon)">Puck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mab_(moon)" title="Mab (moon)">Mab</a> continued the trend), have focused on Herschel's source material. In 1949, the fifth moon, <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, was named by its discoverer <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">Gerard Kuiper</a> after a thoroughly mortal character in Shakespeare's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Tempest_(play)" title="The Tempest (play)">The Tempest</a></i>. The current <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">IAU</a> practice is to name moons after characters from Shakespeare's plays and <i>The Rape of the Lock</i> (although at present only Ariel, Umbriel, and Belinda have names drawn from the latter; all the rest are from Shakespeare). At first, the outermost moons were all named after characters from one play, <i>The Tempest</i>; but with <a href="/wiki/Margaret_(moon)" title="Margaret (moon)">Margaret</a> being named from <i><a href="/wiki/Much_Ado_About_Nothing" title="Much Ado About Nothing">Much Ado About Nothing</a></i> that trend has ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1949_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1949-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg/220px-Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg/330px-Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg/440px-Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masses_of_Uranian_moons-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The relative masses of the Uranian moons. The five rounded moons vary from Miranda at 0.7% to Titania at almost 40% of the total mass. The other moons collectively constitute 0.1%, and are barely visible at this scale.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Rape_of_the_Lock" title="The Rape of the Lock">The Rape of the Lock</a></i> (a poem by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">Alexander Pope</a>):
<ul><li>Ariel, Umbriel, Belinda</li></ul></li>
<li>Plays by <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>:
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i>: Titania, Oberon, Puck</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">The Tempest</a></i>: (Ariel), Miranda, Caliban, Sycorax, Prospero, Setebos, Stephano, Trinculo, Francisco, Ferdinand</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/King_Lear" title="King Lear">King Lear</a></i>: Cordelia</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>: Ophelia</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Taming_of_the_Shrew" title="The Taming of the Shrew">The Taming of the Shrew</a></i>: Bianca</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Troilus_and_Cressida" title="Troilus and Cressida">Troilus and Cressida</a></i>: Cressida</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Othello" title="Othello">Othello</a></i>: Desdemona</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>: Juliet, Mab</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Merchant_of_Venice" title="The Merchant of Venice">The Merchant of Venice</a></i>: Portia</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/As_You_Like_It" title="As You Like It">As You Like It</a></i>: Rosalind</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Much_Ado_About_Nothing" title="Much Ado About Nothing">Much Ado About Nothing</a></i>: Margaret</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Winter%27s_Tale" title="The Winter's Tale">The Winter's Tale</a></i>: Perdita</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Timon_of_Athens" title="Timon of Athens">Timon of Athens</a></i>: Cupid</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> share names with moons of Uranus: <a href="/wiki/171_Ophelia" title="171 Ophelia">171 Ophelia</a>, <a href="/wiki/218_Bianca" title="218 Bianca">218 Bianca</a>, <a href="/wiki/593_Titania" title="593 Titania">593 Titania</a>, <a href="/wiki/666_Desdemona" title="666 Desdemona">666 Desdemona</a>, <a href="/wiki/763_Cupido" title="763 Cupido">763 Cupido</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2758_Cordelia" title="2758 Cordelia">2758 Cordelia</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics_and_groups">Characteristics and groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Characteristics and groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranian_rings_scheme.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1395" decoding="async" height="426" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Uranian_rings_scheme.png/360px-Uranian_rings_scheme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Uranian_rings_scheme.png/540px-Uranian_rings_scheme.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Uranian_rings_scheme.png/720px-Uranian_rings_scheme.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranian_rings_scheme.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of the Uranian moon–ring system</div></div></div>
<p>The Uranian satellite system is the least massive among those of the <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planets</a>. Indeed, the combined mass of the five major satellites is less than half that of <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> (the seventh-largest moon in the Solar System) alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Triton_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Triton-18">[a]</a></sup> The largest of the satellites, Titania, has a radius of 788.9 km,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_1988_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_1988-19">[18]</a></sup> or less than half that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, but slightly more than that of <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, the second-largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, making Titania the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites_by_diameter" title="List of natural satellites by diameter">eighth-largest moon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Uranus is about 10,000 times more massive than its moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Big4Mass_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Big4Mass-20">[b]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_moons">Inner moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Inner moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Inner_moon" title="Inner moon">Inner moon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">Rings of Uranus</a></div>
<p>As of 2016, Uranus is known to have 13 inner moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showalter_Lissauer_2006_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Showalter_Lissauer_2006-12">[12]</a></sup> Their orbits lie inside that of <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>. All inner moons are intimately connected with the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">rings of Uranus</a>, which probably resulted from the fragmentation of one or several small inner moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito2002_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito2002-21">[19]</a></sup> The two innermost moons (<a href="/wiki/Cordelia_(moon)" title="Cordelia (moon)">Cordelia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ophelia_(moon)" title="Ophelia (moon)">Ophelia</a>) are <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_moon" title="Shepherd moon">shepherds</a> of Uranus's ε ring, whereas the small moon <a href="/wiki/Mab_(moon)" title="Mab (moon)">Mab</a> is a source of Uranus's outermost μ ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showalter_Lissauer_2006_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Showalter_Lissauer_2006-12">[12]</a></sup> There may be two additional small (2–7 km in radius) undiscovered shepherd moons located about 100 km exterior to Uranus' <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus#.CE.B1_and_.CE.B2_rings" title="Rings of Uranus">α and β rings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chancia2016_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chancia2016-22">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>At 162 km, <a href="/wiki/Puck_(moon)" title="Puck (moon)">Puck</a> is the largest of the inner moons of Uranus and the only one imaged by <i>Voyager 2</i> in any detail. Puck and Mab are the two outermost inner satellites of Uranus. All inner moons are dark objects; their geometrical <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> is less than 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka2001,_Hubble_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka2001,_Hubble-23">[21]</a></sup> They are composed of water ice contaminated with a dark material, probably radiation-processed organics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DumasSmithTerrile2003_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-DumasSmithTerrile2003-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The small inner moons constantly <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturb</a> each other. The system is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaotic_system" title="Chaotic system">chaotic</a> and apparently unstable. Simulations show that the moons may perturb each other into crossing orbits, which may eventually result in collisions between the moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showalter_Lissauer_2006_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Showalter_Lissauer_2006-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Desdemona_(moon)" title="Desdemona (moon)">Desdemona</a> may collide with either <a href="/wiki/Cressida_(moon)" title="Cressida (moon)">Cressida</a> or <a href="/wiki/Juliet_(moon)" title="Juliet (moon)">Juliet</a> within the next 100 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duncan_Lissauer_1997_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duncan_Lissauer_1997-25">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranian_moon_montage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="2537" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Uranian_moon_montage.jpg/400px-Uranian_moon_montage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Uranian_moon_montage.jpg/600px-Uranian_moon_montage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Uranian_moon_montage.jpg/800px-Uranian_moon_montage.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranian_moon_montage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The five largest moons of Uranus compared at their proper relative sizes and brightnesses. From left to right (in order of increasing distance from Uranus): <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large_moons">Large moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Large moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Uranus has five major moons: <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>. They range in diameter from 472 km for Miranda to 1578 km for Titania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_1988_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_1988-19">[18]</a></sup> All these moons are relatively dark objects: their geometrical albedo varies between 30 and 50%, whereas their <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">Bond albedo</a> is between 10 and 23%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka2001,_Hubble_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka2001,_Hubble-23">[21]</a></sup> Umbriel is the darkest moon and Ariel the brightest. The masses of the moons range from 6.7 × 10<sup>19</sup> kg (Miranda) to 3.5 × 10<sup>21</sup> kg (Titania). For comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> has a mass of 7.5 × 10<sup>22</sup> kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992-26">[24]</a></sup> The major moons of Uranus are thought to have formed in the <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis#Meaning_of_accretion" title="Nebular hypothesis">accretion disc</a>, which existed around Uranus for some time after its formation or resulted from a large impact suffered by <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> early in its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mousis_2004_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mousis_2004-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranusmoonsummer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="696" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Uranusmoonsummer.jpg/260px-Uranusmoonsummer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Uranusmoonsummer.jpg/390px-Uranusmoonsummer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Uranusmoonsummer.jpg/520px-Uranusmoonsummer.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranusmoonsummer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's conception of the Sun's path in the summer sky of a major moon of Uranus (which shares Uranus's axial tilt)</div></div></div>
<p>All major moons comprise approximately equal amounts rock and ice, except Miranda, which is made primarily of ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-29">[27]</a></sup> The ice component may include <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-30">[28]</a></sup> Their surfaces are heavily cratered, though all of them (except Umbriel) show signs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenic" title="Endogenic">endogenic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurfacing" title="Resurfacing">resurfacing</a> in the form of lineaments (canyons) and, in the case of Miranda, ovoid race-track like structures called <a href="/wiki/Corona_(planetary_geology)" title="Corona (planetary geology)">coronae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> Extensional processes associated with upwelling <a href="/wiki/Diapir" title="Diapir">diapirs</a> are likely responsible for the origin of the coronae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pappalardo1997_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pappalardo1997-31">[29]</a></sup> Ariel appears to have the youngest surface with the fewest impact craters, while Umbriel's appears oldest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> A past 3:1 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> between Miranda and Umbriel and a past 4:1 resonance between Ariel and Titania are thought to be responsible for the heating that caused substantial endogenic activity on Miranda and Ariel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tittemore_Wisdom_1990_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tittemore_Wisdom_1990-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tittemore_1990_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tittemore_1990-33">[31]</a></sup>
One piece of evidence for such a past resonance is Miranda's unusually high orbital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclination</a> (4.34°) for a body so close to the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> The largest Uranian moons may be internally differentiated, with rocky <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">cores</a> at their centers surrounded by ice <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-29">[27]</a></sup> Titania and Oberon may harbor liquid water oceans at the core/mantle boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-29">[27]</a></sup> The major moons of Uranus are airless bodies. For instance, Titania was shown to possess no atmosphere at a pressure larger than 10–20 nanobar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The path of the Sun in the local sky over the course of a local day during Uranus's and its major moons' summer solstice is quite different from that seen on most other <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> worlds. The major moons have almost exactly the same rotational axial tilt as Uranus (their axes are parallel to that of Uranus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup> The Sun would appear to follow a circular path around Uranus's celestial pole in the sky, at the closest about 7 degrees from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[c]</a></sup> Near the equator, it would be seen nearly due north or due south (depending on the season). At latitudes higher than 7°, the Sun would trace a circular path about 15 degrees in diameter in the sky, and never set.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg/300px-TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg/450px-TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg/600px-TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheIrregulars_URANUS.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Irregular moons of Uranus. The X axis is labeled in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigametre" title="Gigametre">Gm</a> (million km) and in the fraction of the <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a>'s radius. The eccentricity is represented by the yellow segments (extending from the <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">pericentre to the apocentre</a>) with the inclination represented on the Y axis.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irregular_moons">Irregular moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Irregular moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">Irregular moon</a></div>
<p>As of 2005 Uranus is known to have nine irregular moons, which orbit it at a distance much greater than that of Oberon, the furthest of the large moons. All the irregular moons are probably captured objects that were trapped by Uranus soon after its formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> The diagram illustrates the orbits of those <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">irregular moons</a> discovered so far. The moons above the X axis are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_direct_motion" title="Retrograde and direct motion">prograde</a>, those beneath are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_direct_motion" title="Retrograde and direct motion">retrograde</a>. The radius of the Uranian <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Hill sphere</a> is approximately 73 million km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranus's irregular moons range in size from 120–200 km (<a href="/wiki/Sycorax_(moon)" title="Sycorax (moon)">Sycorax</a>) to about 20 km (<a href="/wiki/Trinculo_(moon)" title="Trinculo (moon)">Trinculo</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> Unlike Jupiter's irregulars, Uranus's show no correlation of axis with <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclination</a>. Instead, the retrograde moons can be divided into two groups based on axis/<a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a>. The inner group includes those satellites closer to Uranus (a &lt; 0.15 r<sub>H</sub>) and moderately eccentric (~0.2), namely <a href="/wiki/Francisco_(moon)" title="Francisco (moon)">Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caliban_(moon)" title="Caliban (moon)">Caliban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephano_(moon)" title="Stephano (moon)">Stephano</a>, and Trinculo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> The outer group (a &gt; 0.15 r<sub>H</sub>) includes satellites with high eccentricity (~0.5): Sycorax, <a href="/wiki/Prospero_(moon)" title="Prospero (moon)">Prospero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Setebos_(moon)" title="Setebos (moon)">Setebos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_(moon)" title="Ferdinand (moon)">Ferdinand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The intermediate inclinations 60° &lt; i &lt; 140° are devoid of known moons due to the <a href="/wiki/Kozai_mechanism" title="Kozai mechanism">Kozai instability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> In this instability region, solar <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbations</a> at apoapse cause the moons to acquire large eccentricities that lead to collisions with inner satellites or ejection. The lifetime of moons in the instability region is from 10 million to a billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Margaret_(moon)" title="Margaret (moon)">Margaret</a> is the only known irregular prograde moon of Uranus, and it currently has the most eccentric orbit of any moon in the Solar System, though Neptune's moon <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">Nereid</a> has a higher mean eccentricity. As of 2008, Margaret's eccentricity is 0.7979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSES_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSES-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List">List</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: List">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-right: 0;">
<caption>Key
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<td style="text-align: center;" width="50"><br/> Inner moons<br/>  
</td>
<td bgcolor="dedeff" style="text-align: center;" width="50"><sup>†</sup> <br/> Major moons<br/>  
</td>
<td bgcolor="d3d3d3" style="text-align: center;" width="50"><sup>‡</sup> <br/> Irregular moons (retrograde)
</td>
<td bgcolor="efefef" style="text-align: center;" width="50"><sup>°</sup> <br/> Irregular moon (prograde)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Uranian moons are listed here by orbital period, from shortest to longest. Moons massive enough for their surfaces to have <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">collapsed</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroid</a> are highlighted in light blue and bolded. Irregular moons with retrograde orbits are shown in dark grey. Margaret, the only known irregular moon of Uranus with a prograde orbit, is shown in light grey.
</p>

<p><i>Sources:</i> NASA/NSSDC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-44">[37]</a></sup> Sheppard, et al. 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> For the recently discovered outer irregular moons (Francisco through Ferdinand) the most accurate orbital data can be generated with the Natural Satellites Ephemeris Service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSES_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSES-38">[35]</a></sup> The irregulars are significantly perturbed by the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Uranus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Triton-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Triton_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The mass of Triton is about 2.14 × 10<sup>22</sup> kg,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyler1989_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyler1989-17">[17]</a></sup> whereas the combined mass of the Uranian moons is about 0.92 × 10<sup>22</sup> kg.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Big4Mass-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Big4Mass_20-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Uranus mass of 8.681 × 10<sup>25</sup> kg / Mass of Uranian moons of 0.93 × 10<sup>22</sup> kg </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> of Uranus is 97°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-2">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Order refers to the position among other moons with respect to their average distance from Uranus.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Label refers to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_numeral" title="Roman numeral">Roman numeral</a> attributed to each moon in order of their discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Diameters with multiple entries such as "<span class="nowrap">60 × 40 × 34</span>" reflect that the body is not a perfect <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroid</a> and that each of its dimensions have been measured well enough. The diameters and dimensions of Miranda, Ariel, Umbriel, and Oberon were taken from Thomas, 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_1988_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_1988-19">[18]</a></sup> The diameter of Titania is from Widemann, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-36">[34]</a></sup> The dimensions and radii of the inner moons are from Karkoschka, 2001,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka2001,_Voyager_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka2001,_Voyager-11">[11]</a></sup> except for Cupid and Mab, which were taken from Showalter, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showalter_Lissauer_2006_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Showalter_Lissauer_2006-12">[12]</a></sup> The radii of outer moons except Sycorax were taken from Sheppard, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2005_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2005-3">[3]</a></sup> The diameter of Sycorax is from Lellouch, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TNOsCool9_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-TNOsCool9-41">[36]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Masses of Miranda, Ariel, Umbriel, Titania, and Oberon were taken from Jacobson, 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992-26">[24]</a></sup> Masses of all other moons were calculated assuming a density of 1.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup> and using given radii.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Negative orbital periods indicate a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a> around Uranus (opposite to the planet's rotation).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Inclination measures the angle between the moon's orbital plane and the plane defined by Uranus's equator.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-d200103-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-d200103_48-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-d200103_48-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Detected in 2001, published in 2003.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
